FACE FACTS: The top screenwriters in Hollywood don't follow this formula any more than an NBA player practices his jump shot on one of those little baskets you hang over the door in your bedroom.  If you want to write a first script, try his method.  If nothing else, it's one way to try and write.  But as you get better, you will realize this is grade school stuff.

B000EHRVP6|5.0|Two Americans and their Icelandic traveling partner take a trip through Eastern Europe to party it up in a remote hostel in Slovakia, where they are drugged and kidnapped only to be sold off to an elite hunting organization that specialized in human torture. After a mediocre debut with CABIN FEVER, Eli Roth burst onto the scene in his sophomore effort HOSTEL, a filthy throwback to the Exploitation films of the 1970's that solidified his name in Horror.<br /><br />HOSTEL, along with other so-called 'torture porn' titles like SAW or THE DEVIL'S REJECTS, has uniformly split Horror audiences based on its use of torture as a main form of entertainment. To say that Roth simply looks to shock and disgust is to grossly misread the entire film in the same way that many critics have dismissed TEXAS CHAINSAW MASSACRE for being too violent. HOSTEL is far more reserved than any of the films that it is most often compared to, and Roth uses implied violence and terror much more often than he does gratuitous gore. Most of the torture sequences are shot off-camera or in cut-aways, leaving the viewer with the victim's twisted facial expressions or screams of anguish. That isn't to say that there isn't gore, because there is, but the gore is only used to accentuate the scenes, and not to define them. True horror always falls secondary to terror in HOSTEL, though this fact is commonly overlooked.<br /><br />The characters of HOSTEL are another brilliant selling point. These are teens that anyone can relate to, and by working outside of the studio system, Roth is able to write his own fluid and naturalistic dialog without the fear of self-censoring himself for political-correctness. This may lead to some questionable vocabulary, but it lends credibility to his characters. In an interesting twist, Roth has given his male leads distinctly effeminate qualities while he victimizes them in the same ways that most female characters are typically treated in this type of picture. People who appear on the side seem inconspicuous and harmless, which allows the audience to let their guard down along with Paxton, Josh, and Oli as they are lured deeper into the lion's den. Jay Hernandez and Derek Richardson are two fun, energetic, and utterly believable characters that draw out a purely empathetic response from viewers that have fully invested in them from early on in the film.<br /><br />Perhaps its greatest strength lies in the mystery behind the sinister organization, itself. Virtually no information is ever revealed about the group or the men that support it, which makes the entire situation that much more intriguing and terrifying. In a genius move, Roth would go back and fully expose the Elite Hunting Group in the film's sequel, even if the attempt was unable to recapture the success of the original.<br /><br />On top of everything else, HOSTEL is a huge crowd-pleasure. Roth caters to a very specific audience, one that revels in gorgeous naked girls and bloody set-pieces. He delivers on every intended level, with a script that is both hilarious at times and painfully excruciating at others. The greatest payoff comes during the revenge sequences, where Roth creates genuinely out-of-your-seat excitement as his characters get their payback against the people that betrayed them.<br /><br />Eli Roth is a Horror fan at heart, and it shows. Though he may have trouble topping this second film, it is obvious that he has a clear control over the genre, and that he has the potential to become one of the next generation Masters of Horror.<br /><br />-Carl Manes<br />I Like Horror Moviesseem inconspicuous and harmless, which allows the audience to let their guard down along with Paxton, Josh, and Oli as they are lured deeper into the lion's den. Jay Hernandez and Derek Richardson are two fun, energetic, and utterly believable characters that draw out a purely empathetic response from viewers that have fully invested in them from early on in the film. Virtually no information is ever revealed about the group or the men that support it, which makes the entire situation that much more intriguing and terrifying. He delivers on every intended level, with a script that is both hilarious at times and painfully excruciating at others. The greatest payoff comes during the revenge sequences, where Roth creates genuinely out-of-your-seat excitement as his characters get their payback against the people that betrayed them.    Eli Roth is a Horror fan at heart, and it shows. Though he may have trouble topping this second film, it is obvious that he has a clear control over the genre, and that he has the potential to become one of the next generation Masters of Horror.     -Carl Manes  I Like Horror Movies

0756404738|5.0|It's hard to write a review after reading a book like this. It's like reviewing a sunset after a spring storm. Yes, that's a poetic image, and that's what Rothfuss does to your brain. I plan on selling all my worldly possessions soon and devoting my life to chasing the wind...<br /><br />But meanwhile, I'll try to do this book justice. It starts much as Name of the Wind ended, with Kvothe still a 15 year old at the University. It spends about 300 pages in this mode. Kvothe has a handful of new adventures at the university and tells us much about his classes and his never ending pining over Denna.<br /><br />At this point it's good to make a, um, point. The Kingkiller Chronicles are not about the main storyline, i.e. Kvothe discovering who killed his parents, why, hunting the killers down, and avenging them. Many readers will no doubt be impatient at the amount of time Rothfuss spends describing seemingly unimportant side adventures of Kvothe's. The book is about how a man becomes a legend. What would seem to be the main storyline is just as incidental as all the rest. It's nice to have that main plot line running through the many hundreds of pages of his adventures, and it does give Kvothe's life a bit of a forward thrust. But that is not what the book is about. So if you read this series hoping to get a page turner as he chases down the Chandrian, then you may be disappointed. (Though it is still a page turner).<br /><br />This reminds me of when Kvothe tells a story to his two friends. At the end of the story they're a little confused because the story didn't follow the familiar pattern they expected. Then Kvothe explained that he had told them a story that his people would tell each other, not a story meant for a general audience.<br /><br />I wonder if there is a message from Pat in this.<br /><br />Instead what we have is a man at the end of his life, or what he hopes is the end of his life, recounting how his legend was born. And that legend is built of pieces. This book gives us some of those pieces.<br /><br />He eventually leaves the University for most of a year. Rothfuss deftly skips over some of the more irrelevant side adventures, a trial and a shipwreck, and stays focused on those things which begin to build Kvothe as a person - his training, battles, moral decisions, role as a leader.<br /><br />Kvothe is a bit of a contradiction. On the one hand, no matter where he goes, he's always a fish out of water. He's always the different one, the outcast, the landless, lowborn Edema. On the other hand, he's able to adapt wherever he goes, no matter who he is with. He can find a place anywhere. This contradiction is brilliantly balanced by Rothfuss, and we see that perhaps they're different sides of the same coin. His freedom gives him nothing to lose.<br /><br />The strength of the book, though, is probably the sheer poetry of its prose. I don't think I've ever believed magic was as real since maybe watching Star Wars and the force for the first time as a 5 year old. I've often had difficult conversations with friends who were of a very strict, scientific and rational mindset. Trying to get them to accept the truth of other ways of seeing the world has been futile. I think from now own I'll simply recommend this book to them. If they can understand how Kvothe sees the wind, then they'll finally know what I've been telling them. Rothfuss does a better job of describing the worldview of the slightly mad artist/poet and making it magic than anything I've ever read.<br /><br />This book, and the entire series, is destined to be a classic.<br /><br />I somehow survived the four year wait between books one and two. I have no idea how I'll last until book three.. This book gives us some of those pieces.<br /><br />He eventually leaves the University for most of a year. B0000VD04M|5.0|Despite his best efforts, Ed Wood's name has become synonymous with terrible filmmaking, but now, Tim Burton gives the quirky and ambitious director his moment in the spotlight! ED WOOD is both sad and uplifting, and shows the best and worst sides that Hollywood and stardom have to offer. The comedic tale follows poor Ed as he struggles endlessly to live out his dreams of directing movies that are filled with action and imagination. His biggest failings lead him to chance encounters with genre legends Bela Lugosi and Orson Welles, who continue to inspire him even when all hope seems lost. On top of everything else, his love for angora sweaters and cross-dressing continue to set him back both professionally and personally. If enthusiasm had only equaled talent, Ed Wood may have become the world's greatest director, and Tim Burton is sure to show it throughout this heartwarming tragedy.<br /><br />Johnny Depp stars as Ed Wood in a stylized but highly entertaining exaggeration of the character. He delivers many subtle nuances to the role that make him pitifully naive and endearing at the same time. Depp is joined by a talented cast, including Sarah Jessica Parker and Patricia Arquette as his two love interests, Jeffrey Jones and Bill Murray as his kooky cohorts, and Martin Landau in an Academy Award-winning performance as the tattered old Bela Lugosi, with whom Wood would share a close friendship throughout his final years. Landau is fully deserving of all critical praise, as he is impeccable in the role and completely indistinguishable from the real-life Lugosi. Wood and Lugosi have many common bonds that draw them together, particularly in that they have strong motivations and inspiration, but that they are each critically flawed in numerous ways.<br /><br />The characters are drawn together in a colorful story as Wood brainlessly cons investors into supporting his artless productions. Their adventures lead them into unbelievably funny situations where they are run out of studios and left out to film in the dark without sound or working props. Burton capitalizes on the most outrageous moments of Wood's career, but equally balances the silly humor with dramatic lulls to link the film with reality. The uncanny reproductions from films like GLEN OR GLENDA, BRIDE OF THE MONSTER, and (of course) PLAN 9 FROM OUTER SPACE are pulled off flawlessly, as are the casting decisions for side characters like Vampira and Tor Johnson.<br /><br />ED WOOD is essential viewing for all movie fans whether they are familiar with the character or not. It encapsulates the entrepreneurial spirit of the independent filmmaker and the inherent difficulties in following the golden road to Hollywood with more than a hint of satire. One cannot help but be drawn back to Wood's terrible films after watching this loving tribute!<br /><br />-Carl Manes<br />I Like Horror Moviesd left out to film in the dark without sound or working props. I failed to look up instantly, continuing to focus upon my grad school reading at the time. Initially, \\"I don't know this 80's song,\\" was the only fleeting thought across my mind. As the unsuspecting chorus engaged, however, I could no longer ignore the calling. \\"What IS this?,\\" I strongly inquired to an empty room. I was almost angry that I did not recognize a genuine new-wave classic (PS - Yes, New Wave is a very real genre. End the debate). In viewing the little Pandora player, I was inundated by 3 lines of nonsense: \\"Kim & Jessie\\"; \\"m83\\"; \\"Saturdays=Youth.\\" Research ensued, uncovering the jarring reality that this was, indeed, a CURRENT band.<br /><br />Following a brief MySpace listen, the purchase was on it's way. Subsequent to receipt of tangible CD (yes, I believe in those, too), the tunes nary left the headphones or space of my experiences. Do any of you recall those formative albums, where the listen ushers in instant transportation of nostalgic times? Akin to the the way the scent of apple-cinnamon takes one back to grandma's house as a child, certain albums were crafted with such heart and thoughtfulness that they capture the spirit of one's life transition of the time. Careening through nostalgic keys and dream-wave melodies, this is NOT reminiscent of the 80's in a power-cheese manner. New wave stirs the soul? Undoubtedly. While initially odd, the title of the album resounds well with the spirit of the album. Saturday WAS once the freedom of existence. The songs whisper to the heart's longings to the lost innocence of past. The album is, really, a lament.. 0316098337|5.0|\\"Room\\" is a novel of tremendous originality and poignancy. As a boy of five who has lived his entire life in captivity with his kidnapped mother, Jack's life in Room is his \\"normal.\\"  His Ma uses her considerable creativity to devise games to teach him and encourage him creatively, without letting on that the things they read about and see on TV are real and just waiting outside Room's metal door. Room is his entire world, and Ma does everything in her ability to enrich his life there, while shielding him at all costs from \\"Old Nick,\\" her captor who comes to visit most nights after 9:00, after Jack is put to bed in Wardrobe. As time goes on, though, Jack's curiosity and questions remind Ma that she can't keep shielding him from reality forever, and their life in Room cannot continue on indefinitely. She and Jack must combine their knowledge and creativity to come up with a plan.    I found this book fascinating all the way through. An adult novel from a little boy's perspective? High-concept, but it is brilliantly done. Although Jack is overall a happy boy, he is confused by the disturbing things he hears in Room when he is shut in Wardrobe after dark. Readers are gradually made aware of the frightening things that mostly fly over Jack's head because he thinks they're either normal or part of a dream.     Although the turning point of the novel occurs about halfway through, I don't agree that it loses steam thereafter. After this point in the book, one wonders what could possibly happen next, but it turns out that the major event midway through the book creates many additional challenges for both of the main characters, making the whole thing a riveting read. As for the criticism that \\"Old Nick\\" is an underdeveloped character--that is just not what this book is about. It's not about the backstory of a predator, it's about Ma's love for her son (and vice versa) and the challenges that Room imposes on both their lives. Jack is telling the story and he'll tell you allyou want to know about the pictures on their walls, their craft-friends Eggsnake, Labyrinth, etc., but since Ma protects him from having to see or speak to \\"Old Nick,\\" Jack only describes the bits and pieces he sees and hears at night.   You won't be able to put it down. Read \\"Room!\\"

I wouldn't call this a horror film, but more of a dramatic thriller. It follows a young boy named Owen, a skinny, shy, socially awkward twelve year old. His young life is in shambles: his parents are divorcing, he doesn't have any friends, and he's being bullied in school. The only thing in his life that makes him happy are Now & Laters. But then Abby moves in next door, a mysterious young girl who finds Owen just as curious as he finds her. They slowly strike up a friendship, and through her Owen starts to take a little bit of control in his life. Yet something doesn't seem right about Abby, and Owen must decide if he wants to let that get in the way of his friendship with the young girl Abby.<br /><br />Let Me In doesn't pull many punches in terms of content. It is brutal and bloody. It has quite a few mature situations. And it carries with every scene a brooding and dark atmosphere. I guess that's to be expected though when it comes to real vampire films.<br /><br />I can highly recommend this movie to any fan of quality horror or thriller films. B003S0IJWA|4.0|Bernstein recorded the Beethoven symphonies with the Vienna Philharmonic for DG during the late 1970s to much acclaim.  Those performances quickly overshadowed Bernstein's earlier set with the NYPO recorded for Columbia in the 1960s.  There is much to recommend in this earlier survey of the symphonies however, collected by Sony Classical here along with a set of overtures and a great performance of Beethoven's Violin Concerto with Isaac Stern as soloist.  These performances have an exuberance, energy, and scrappy, rough-hewn quality that is missing in the more refined and sometimes stodgy Vienna set.  For example, the recording of the Eroica symphony in this NY collection has a fire and verve missing from the often stately, overly monumental foray with Vienna.  Of particular worth in this set are the performances of the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, 4th, and 7th symphonies.  I feel the one disappointment is the performance of the 9th Symphony, which lacks the drama that characterized his later Vienna recording of the 9th (and the other performances in this collection for that matter).<br /><br />In short, fans of Bernstein and Beethoven will be well served by this collection and should find much to enjoy.

This is not an action-packed book by any account. For example, there's a homoerotic scene between Eilis and her coworker, a secret marriage, racial tensions, and more.    Overall, I enjoyed this book and want to explore Coibin's other works. If you're in the market for a leisurely piece of historical fiction, check _Brooklyn_ out.

B004BLJQOK|5.0|**REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS**    Let Me In is the American adaptation of the 2008 Swedish vampire film, Let The Right One In (which I haven't seen for any basis of comparison, although I have heard awesome things about it). Rated at 89% on the Rotten Tomatoes tomatometer, Let Me In did not disappoint on either the horror front or the romance front. It's not your typical vampire film, nor is it your typical boy meets girl love story. Instead, it is an amazing combination of the two, producing a compelling movie experience that is dark and terrible as it is sweet.    Owen (played by Kodi Smit-McPhee from The Road) is a young boy who gets bullied at school and spends his time entertaining himself alone in the courtyard of his apartment building. One evening, two mysterious people show up in at night and move into the building - a young girl and her father. But Abby (played by Chloe Grace Moretz from KickAss) is no ordinary twelve-year-old girl, she is a centuries year old vampire. Despite her warning to Owen that they can't be friends, they are drawn together by a shared loneliness and a relationship starts to form. The core of this film really centers around these two young people - their dialogue, their shared silent glances, their emotions, and their budding feelings for each other. Their relationship is dark and sweet because you can't help becoming invested in them and what will essentially be a doomed romance, because Abby is what she is. On that note, I thought it was incredibly poignant when we learn exactly who Abby's father is, and know that that will also be Owen's fate, should he choose to stay with Abby.    Sweetness aside, this film also has enough horror and shocking moments to have had me covering my eyes. Abby is terrifying as a quick-moving, evil-eyed vampire with her snarling blood-drenched mouth, leaving you in no doubt of the creature that she is. She rips into flesh like nobody's business, snapping necks and tearing bloody chunks out with her teeth. Even Abby's father who garbs himself from head to toe in a black garbage bag is creepy and frightening when he goes hunting for Abby. The scarier parts of this movie only add dimension to the sweeter parts, like Abby and Owen's interaction, and it just makes you feel for them more because there's no \\"happy ever after\\" for them. He'll kill for her, protect her, feed her, and she'll leave him behind when he gets too old ... just like the boy-turned-old-man before him. It's so tragic.    I thought Abby was a complex and fascinating character. She drew me in because she's doomed to be a child forever trapped in the life of this monstrous vampire, and a lot of how she is comes across as very childlike. For me, she was as good as Kirsten Dunst in Interview With A Vampire. Like Kirsten, she played the vampire versus the child amazingly well - the violent growling at her father for not getting any blood versus the sweetness of her normal girl voice is incredible. Her ability to be both monster and innocent was phenomenal. I don't think she's manipulative and scheming as suggested by other reviewers in terms of looking for someone else to take care of her. She tried to leave Owen for his own good, and ends up coming back to save his life. So that for me, erased the whole thought that her actions were contrived to make Owen fall in love with her. I think she also fell in love with him, and she knows that he protected her, too.    Owen was also a complex character, although in a different way, almost an opposite way to Abby. He's a powerless bullied kid who wants to awaken the monster inside of him - he wants to punish his tormenters. This is evident when he's stabbing the tree with a penknife, hissing \\"you scared, little girl?\\" It's interesting because he's afraid of a few kids, but yet he's able to face and even love the utter terror of a creature like a vampire.    I especially loved the juxtaposition of Let Me In with Romeo and Juliet, which Owen was reading in school,in terms of highlighting the beauty and tragedy of their relationship. Let Me In is a beautiful and terrifying film. I couldn't stop thinking about it for hours after I'd seen it, especially about Owen's eventual choice and the cost of such a choice. It was both bittersweet and chilling.    Superbly acted and one of the best vampire movies I've ever seen, I would rate Let Me In 5 out of 5 stars.

B00005ABX0|2.0|Maybe it would be best to just pretend 1967 never even happened for The Beach Boys.  It SHOULD have been the year they placed themselves in the \\"album as art\\" league with the rest of the heavyweights (they already had, or Brian had, with \\"Pet Sounds\\" in '66 but 1967 was the year of technicolor explosion for every major band).  It should have been the year that Brian and the Boys released their masterpiece \\"SMiLE\\" and then performed it in it's entirety at The Monterey International Pop Festival that June.  It should have been, but it wasn't.  Instead it was the year Brian finally broke down, The Beach Boys pulled out of their scheduled appearance at Monterey, and then released two hastily recorded albums only three months apart that were night and day in comparison!  And neither of them made up for the disappointment that the rock world felt when they took the place of \\"SMiLE.\\"  After 1967, The Beach Boys would be viewed in an entirely different light and not a very good one at that.  The band's September 1967 release \\"Smiley Smile\\" is a train wreck of an album, unfocused and under produced (and you thought \\"Mardi Gras\\" by CCR was bad).  The bands next release that followed in December of 1967, \\"Wild Honey,\\" is an attempt at a soul infused album but, despite a few good tracks, falls flat.  It might have saved The Beach Boys a bit of critical backlash if they had just released \\"Wild Honey\\" and skipped \\"Smiley Smile\\" altogether.  \\"Smiley Smile\\" is akin to a dinner party you've spent weeks preparing for, and when it all goes to pot at the very last minute you're forced to whip up something quick an hour or two before the guests arrive!  And it's nowhere near the masterpiece you had in mind!  \\"SMiLE\\" was going to take The Beach Boys to the next level and Brian held the key to getting them through that door.  The problem with \\"Smiley Smile\\" is that it's production lapse doesn't sound intentional, it just sounds like a band who at this point, doesn't care WHAT they're doing.  Capitol Records included \\"Good Vibrations\\" on the album in order to save it, and that song (along with \\"Heroes And Villains\\") are the only two slightly redeeming factors to the whole record.  I know there are fans out there of both of these records, and that's all fine and dandy.  But for most of us, it's too hard to separate these albums from what could have been but ultimately WASN'T.    However, I'm not going to tell Beach Boys fans NOT to buy this two-fer, just be prepared for what you might be in for.  So here's a run down of \\"The Good, The Average, and The Awful\\" for both albums:  SMILEY SMILE - THE GOOD:  \\"Heroes And Villains\\" and \\"Good Vibrations.\\"  THE AVERAGE:  \\"She's Goin' Bald,\\" \\"With Me Tonight,\\" \\"Wind Chimes,\\" \\" Gettin' Hungry,\\" and \\"Wonderful.\\"  THE AWFUL:  \\"Vegetables,\\" \\"Fall Breaks And Back To Winter,\\" \\"Little Pad,\\" and \\"Whistle In.\\"  WILD HONEY - THE GOOD:  \\"Wild Honey,\\" \\"Aren't You Glad,\\" \\"Darlin',\\" \\"I'd Love Just Once To See You,\\" \\"Let The Wind Blow,\\" \\"How She Boogalooed It,\\" and \\"Mama Says.\\"  THE AVERAGE:  \\"Country Air,\\" \\"A Thing Or Two,\\" and \\"Here Comes The Night.\\"  THE AWFUL:  \\"I Was Made To Love Her.\\"    EPILOGUE - As you know (and if you don't, well, you will now), the original \\"SMiLE,\\" put together as best as humanly possible by Mark Linett, is coming out on Nov. 1st of this year!  Five tracks have been leaked in various ways, both by You Tube and The Beach Boys Facebook page, and all five sound amazing.  The new mix of \\"Vega-Tables\\" blows the \\"Smiley Smile\\" version out of the water and across the landscape!  The new mixes of \\"Heroes And Villians,\\" \\"Cabinessence,\\" and \\"Wonderful\\" sound as they should have back in 1967.  \\"Good Vibrations\\" is even better too!  The 11-1-11 release of \\"SMiLE\\" is either going to do one of two things with fans perception of \\"Smiley Smile.\\"  It will either A) completely turn fans off of the record to where they'll never be able to listen to it again or B) make them accept \\"Smiley Smile\\" and think of it as a sort of loose, give-away album that The Beach Boys threw together in wake of releasing their masterpiece in the summer of '67.  Had The Beach Boys released \\"SMiLE\\" back in '67, \\"Wild Honey\\" could have followed it honestly as many major bands then were scaling down after reaching their psychedelic zenith.  But instead of climbing to the top of the mountain as The Beatles did, The Beach Boys chose to go around it, and back then it didn't work that way.  Hopefully Nov. 1st will restore fans who kinda let go of the band when \\"Smiley Smile\\" came out decades ago.

031261246X|5.0|I write technology and network security books, articles, newsletters and white papers for a living. Reading for fun is one of my guilty pleasures. But lately I've been disappointed in the deteriorating quality of the fiction books that I read. Even many of my long-time favorite novelists seem to have gotten sloppy, and most of the new books are badly written from a technical point of view, fail to bring anything fresh to overused plotlines, and/or aren't able to keep the story moving, make me care about the characters or wrap up the plot elements into a coherent whole.<br /><br />There are, however, a few folks who can still tell a story. The two books I read immediately preceding Zero Day were by Neal Stephenson and Orson Scott Card, old standbys who never let me down. Those guys are tough acts to follow, and as I opened up Mark's book (in the Kindle app on my Galaxy Tab, if anyone cares), I felt a mixture of eager anticipation and a smidgen of worry.  Mostly, I was excited to embark on reading a novel written by someone I actually knew personally; that doesn't happen every day.<br /><br />If you don't know who Mark Russinovich is, check out his bio on Wikipedia. Pretty impressive technical creds, huh? I knew that Mark really knows his stuff when it comes to computers, so I was looking forward to (finally!) reading a novel about technology that wouldn't have me stopping every few pages to shake my head and say \\"no, no, no - it doesn't work that way.\\" (Dan Brown's Digital Fortress is just one example of a technothriller that, despite the author's storytelling skills, I couldn't enjoy because I wasn't able to get past the technical mistakes).<br /><br />The worry? I knew it was a first novel, and I know very well that being very, very smart doesn't necessarily ensure that a person can write in a compelling way. Writing fiction is very different from writing technical papers. And in fact, some of the smartest people I know write some of the most boring material. And I also knew, from my own efforts, that writing a novel is hard work. Writing a novel well is even harder. Would he be able to pull it off?<br /><br />I'm going to be brutally honest here: The opening scene didn't exactly impress me and make me want to read more.  Oh, I know starting things off with some gratuitous sex is supposed to be a sure-fire technique for grabbing readers' attention and it probably works just fine with the young male geeks who are likely to make up a large part of this book's audience. But if you aren't a member of that group and you don't really particularly like that sort of thing, hang in there. It's all over in a page and a half and once we get that out of the way, the story starts to take off into the wild blue yonder. Literally. Of course, John Nance is one of my favorite novelists and aviation is one of my special interests so I'm always a sucker for a good barely-averted-air-disaster scene. Scene 2, played out in the cockpit of British Airways Flight 188, had me hooked by the third paragraph. And from there on out, it was a wild and wooly ride.<br /><br />As I made my way through the book, it only got better and better. Many years ago, I devoured all the popular novels written by Arthur Hailey, whose Airport and Runway Zero-Eight were my favorites (aviation buff, remember).  His other titles, such as Hotel (about the hospitality industry), Wheels (about the automobile industry), The Moneychangers (about the banking industry), and Strong Medicine (about the pharmaceutical industry) also kept me up late for many nights in the 70s and 80s. Zero Day is written in the same format as Hailey's books, shuffling the scene back and forth between many different localities and characters, with one strong protagonist tying it all together.  Great literature? No - but highly entertaining and also informative, thanks to all the research that went into them. Is it a formulaic style? Maybe so, but it's an effective one. It can also be a maddening one ifit's not handled just right - I've read more than one book written in this style that fell apart completely because the author couldn't seem to juggle the plethora of subplots or keep all those characters' personalities consistent. I'm happy to report that Mark did a great job of managing this shifting landscape.<br /><br />The plot is built around a rapidly evolving set of incidents involving computer system failures that result in serious situations, some of which are only costly in monetary terms, but many of which are life-threatening. Our emergent hero is Jeff Aiken, mild-mannered computer expert who left the frustrations and bureaucratic red tape of his government job (in the CIA, no less) for the private sector, where he makes lots of money doing damage control and clean-up after corporate security breaches, somewhat akin to the way Red Adair sweeps into town to put things right after an oilwell fire. Jeff and his former government colleague, Dr. Daryl Haugen (the somewhat stereotypical beautiful blonde computer genius who wants to be admired for her brains instead of her body) come to realize that the incidents - ranging from the decimation of a law firm's network to the failure of a 787 jetliner's computerized systems to a \\"glitch\\" in a hospital's records system that causes several patient deaths from improper medication - are all related and part of a cyberterrorist attack.  The race to track down the bad guys and prevent the worst from happening is on.<br /><br />The story is deadly serious, and it will keep you awake, turning the pages late into the night, as you worry over what's going to happen in the book (and may also keep you awake nights after you've finished it, worrying about just how close we are to a real-life version of these events). There are also moments of comic relief. As the male chauvinist DHS bureaucrat - who's involved in some clandestine activities of his own - watches  Daryl walk away, he ruminates over how \\"these computer types were always getting worked up over nothing\\" - and how \\"the few attractive women among them were the worst.\\"  As a woman in the male-dominated computer security field, I can't help grinning and thinking I've met this guy before - a few times over.<br /><br />When I read a novel, especially a first novel, and more especially a first novel about computer-related matters written by a computer expert, I can't help wondering how much of himself the author put into his main character.  Jeff taught at Carnegie-Mellon; Mark got his Ph.D. from the same university. Jeff is an entrepreneur who has built a successful business tracking down malware; Mark co-founded Winternals Software. They say every first novel is autobiographical to some extent, so it's hard not to see Jeff as a fictionalized version of Mark. But that only adds to the mystique.<br /><br />All in all, it's a great read. If you want to get nit-picky, there are enough typos to be annoying - as is the case with almost every novel I read these days, including those by big name authors such as Stephen King and John Grisham.  These are little things, such as \\"then\\" when it should have been \\"than\\" or missing punctuation.  Some are the types of mistakes that get introduced in the editing process. Darn, how I wish these publishers would let me proofread these books before they go to print (hint, hint).<br /><br />I noticed that one reviewer complained about getting bogged down in the technical details. Maybe it's because I'm in the tech industry and already have an understanding of these things, but I thought the technical issues were explained very well, translating the techno jargon to mainstream terms and using analogies that should be easy for anyone, tech savvy or not, to understand. If you're an IT pro, you'll get into it on several different levels - but don't be afraid to recommend it to your friends and relatives who aren't computer professionals (Do, however, be prepared to answer their concerned questions aboutwhether all this could really happen when they find themselves scared to death by what takes place in the novel).<br /><br />At $14.08 for the hardcover or $11.99 for the electronic edition (on Amazon), this book is a bargain, for both its entertainment and its educational value. Don't just buy one; buy a second one for a friend.\\"the few attractive women among them were the worst.\\"  As a woman in the male-dominated computer security field, I can't help grinning and thinking I've met this guy before - a few times over. 0205313426|5.0|This book is worth reading several times. Reading advice on style has caused me to think explicitly about style when writing, even if I am not thinking about exactly what Strunk and White said. This is like how after carefully reading a book on manners one might not remember every rule in the book, but might then think more about manners and thus be more courteous.<br /><br />The opinions of authors who have thought long and deeply about a topic are worth reading, even if one doesn't agree with all the opinions.<br /><br />The only rule that could cause problems is Rule 17, \\"Omit needless words\\". I think it takes experience to apply this rule well, and I recommend that a beginning writer have someone whose style they respect check their work to see what should be cut down and where more explanation or elaboration is needed.<br /><br />This is not a book of grammar. For that I like Otto Jespersen's \\"Essentials of English Grammar\\".<br /><br />White's essay \\"An approach to style\\" itself makes the entire book worth buying. \\"The language is perpetually in flux: it is a living stream, shifting, changing, receiving new strength from a thousand tributaries, losing old forms in the backwaters of time. To suggest that a young writer not swim in the main stream of this turbulence would be foolish indeed, and such is not the intent of these cautionary remarks. The intent is to suggest that in choosing between the formal and the informal, the regular and the offbeat, the general and the special, the orthodox and the heretical, the beginner err on the side of conservatism, on the side of established usage. No idiom is taboo, no accent forbidden; there is simply a better chance of doing well if the writer holds a steady course, enters the stream of English quietly, and does not thrash about.\\"

What follows is a series of elaborate illusions, sabotages, seductions, and finally murder as each magician tries to ruin the other while seeking to discover the secrets behind the other's illusions. Anne had a strained relationship with her mother Edith, and clearly gravitated toward her beloved Pim (father). However, one of the problems I had with this book was that I felt that these character's voices were not developed enough. A few times I would be reading and forget whose point of view it was, Sam's or Grace's, because both character's voices sounded the same to me. For a good part of the book I wondered \\"Why don't they just move somewhere hot if the cold makes them change?\\" Grace, who is supposed to be a very smart straight A student never asks Sam about this, (which to me would have been a blaringly obvious question), when she talks to him about a cure. This issue isn't addressed until much later in the book, when another character finally asks Sam about it. He says that some of the wolves had tried it before but it hadn't worked; they had only become very sensitive to subtle temperature changes. This didn't make much sense to me either. But the thing that I really didn't understand, was the theory Grace came up with for a 'cure'--that inducing a fever while in human form would kill the wolf inside.       I went with all of this anyways because I liked the characters and I was enjoying the story, but this is why I can only give this book 4 stars. In my opinion the plot needed a clearer sense of direction, and all of the werewolf transformation/cure stuff (which is really the backbone of the whole story) should have been better explained or the theories better developed and strengthened so that they were more solid.       Despite the flaws I still very much enjoyed Shiver, and I would recommend it to others. I eagerly started Linger the moment I finished Shiver, and I'm already anticipating the third book, Forever, due out in July. With its likeable characters, sweet romance, and deliciously beautiful writing, Shiver is definitely a book worth reading.

B004G5ZXVQ|4.0|As with \\"Reveal,\\" \\"Around the Sun,\\" and \\"Accelerate,\\" I approached the release of \\"Collapse Into Now\\" with apprehension, doubt, and a sliver of hope. Hope that this would be the record I would enjoy from start to finish. Hope that this record would sound like the R.E.M. I knew from the early 1980s through the mid-1990s. Hope that this would be an album I'd still be enthusiastic about after a few listens and a few weeks or even months.    When I first heard \\"Discoverer\\" a few months ago, I prepared myself for disappointment. However, for me the album as a whole works better than any of R.E.M.'s efforts since \\"New Adventures in Hi-Fi.\\" \\"Up\\" and \\"Reveal\\" seemed like experimental efforts by the \\"three-legged dog\\" version of R.E.M. To my ears those records seem like the work of a band trying to discover its identity and dealing with the emotional aftermath from losing integral fourth member, Bill Berry, who retired. There's good music on both records, but, as a long-time fan, I'm not big on R.E.M. songs without guitars and drums. I don't know what \\"Around the Sun\\" was other than a cud that served as a wake-up call to the bandmembers. There are a couple of good songs on that record, but for the most part it's forgettable. I don't think I've ever been as doubtful about the band as I was after that effort. I almost gave up on them.    With \\"Accelerate\\" came promises of a \\"return to form.\\" I refused to buy into the hype until I spent some quality time with the record. Though it \\"rocked\\" and included more guitar and drums, I got bored with it quickly. The rockers seemed nearly identical, the lyrics were awful, and the whole thing seemed like the work of a band that was trying to hard. I probably haven't listened to \\"Accelerate\\" in its entirety in a year and a half. So when I heard similar \\"return to form\\" assurances in advance of \\"Collapse Into Now,\\" I refused to get excited.     Then I heard the entire record and fell in love. Even \\"Discoverer,\\" as the lead track, grew on mequickly. Think of all the things that make R.E.M. what it is - jangly guitars, beautiful Stipe/Mills harmonies, mandolin, lush ballads, guitar-driven rockers, silly punk/pop songs dripping with attitude and lyrics bordering on jibberish, and spoken word stream of consciousness poetry. Several guest appearances - though subdued - add to the songs. Peaches really makes \\"Alligator_Aviator_Autopilot_Antimatter,\\" reminding the listener of Kate Pierson on \\"Shiny Happy People\\" and \\"Me in Honey.\\"     I've read a few reviews accusing R.E.M. of sounding too much like R.E.M. on this record. I wonder how many of those critics bashed R.E.M. for not sounding like R.E.M. on \\"Up,\\" \\"Reveal,\\" and \\"Around the Sun.\\" I've been waiting since 1996 for another R.E.M. record on which R.E.M. sounds like R.E.M. When a band's back catalog is as strong as R.E.M.'s, I see no sin in borrowing some of the highlights from its own history in creating new music. For a band, after 30 years and 15 records, to make a record this consistently good is something for which Messrs. Stipe, Buck, and Mills should be proud.       If you enjoyed \\"Green,\\" \\"Out of Time,\\" \\"Automatic for the People,\\" \\"Monster,\\" or \\"New Adventures in Hi-Fi,\\" then there's something for you on this album.    Favorite tracks: \\"Discoverer,\\" \\"Uberlin,\\" \\"It Happened Today,\\" \\"Walk It Back,\\" \\"Alligator_Aviator_Autopilot_Antimatter,\\" & \\"Me, Marlon Brando, Marlon Brando and I.\\"

0312600844|4.0|This is a story about family - about the presence and absence of relationships (both functional and dysfunctional) - set in modern America, and told from a number of different perspectives.  The novel opens with an historic view of the Berglund family seen from the perspective of their neighbours in St Paul, Minnesota. The Berglunds (husband Walter, wife Patty, and children Jessica and Joey), are a liberal middle-class family who were part of the gentrification of urban St Paul.  Patty was a homemaker, and an ideal neighbour, Walter was an environmentally conscious lawyer.  On the face of it, an ideal family but a closer look at the Berglund's lives reveals that all is not as it seems.  Patty's much loved son Joey becomes involved with a neighbour's daughter, Connie, and moves in with Connie and her mother.  Why has this happened? Who are the Berglunds, really, and where do they come from and what do they stand for? What are they seeking?  Why do the family relocate to Washington DC, and leave the home that they have worked on for years?<br /><br />`Then again, there had always been something not quite right about the Berglunds.'<br /><br />The next part of the story is an unpublished autobiography composed by Patty Berglund at the suggestion of her therapist.  In this, we learn of Patty's youth as a star basketball player and of events in her past.  Of how she meets an attractive musician named Richard Katz, and his room-mate Walter Berglund, and of the events that follow in her life.<br />The novel then moves to New York in 2004 and is seen through the rotating third-person perspectives of Richard, Joey and Walter. Their overlapping narratives take us through much of the novel, until an addendum to Patty's autobiography brings us to 2010 and almost to the end of the story.<br /><br />The structure of the novel works well to convey the themes of the novel, but summarising the story in more detail would diminish the impact of reading it.  An element of surprise is needed in order to appreciate the story as it unfolds. Experiencing some of the same scenes form different perspectives enables us to see varying levels of significance, especially where one character's story is interrupted at a critical point, and another character's story begins.<br /><br />And the characters?  While the major characters are well-defined and generally consistent with the stereotypes they represent (I especially enjoyed Joey) I found Patty, frustrating and not particularly likeable or interesting. And because so much of the book is about Patty, this impacted on my overall enjoyment.<br /><br />I admired the writing in this novel.  I appreciated the focus on both the way in which the nature of American society is evolving, as well as the way in which changing (self-perpetuating but frequently circular) relationships in families define relative constructs of freedom.  But the major theme is about freedom: how do we define it, and how will we recognise it?  Is it tangible or intangible; relative or absolute; singular or collective?  What is freedom, if we don't (or can't) use it? What is the purpose of being free if we can't enjoy it?<br /><br />It's possible to just read and enjoy this book, but there's plenty of food for further thought for those so inclined.<br /><br />Jennifer Cameron-Smithe the story as it unfolds. I appreciated the focus on both the way in which the nature of American society is evolving, as well as the way in which changing (self-perpetuating but frequently circular) relationships in families define relative constructs of freedom. The 2nd Concerto has been recorded by Heifetz and Menuhin, among others, but it is not such a strong work.      3.  The Concerto No. 3 shocked me when I first heard this recording on LP when it came out around 1980.  I am a violinist and pretty familiar with the standard repertoire, and I was startled that such a fine work was played by nobody.   Accardo's recording was the first.      The first movement is heroic, if a bit bombastic in places.  The Adagio second movement is lovely, noble and romantic.  The soloist leads with an introduction before the orchestra announces the main theme, which the soloist elaborates on. The soft horn calls near the end of the movement are something to hear.  The finale is a lively tarantella that is, to me, more exciting and more interesting than the finale of the Concerto No. 1.      4. One reviewer mentioned that the violin sounds better than in Accardo's other records.  That's true, for two reasons.  First, the recording engineers outdid themselves on this set.  The sound is rich, well balanced and has a beautiful sheen to it.  The other reason is the violin.  Accardo owned a 1733 Guarneri del Ges violin for a while  (he has owned several Stradivaris).  The LP album covers of these recordings show him holding the Guarneri.  From the timbre it certainly sounds like he was using it for this set.  Anyway, it sounds extraordinary on these recordings--rich, brilliant and bit husky on a few low notes.  You won't find many recordings of solo violin music that will match this for beauty of sound.

1401309666|4.0|When I started reading The Long Tail by Chris Anderson, I did not fully understand the concept of the book.  The cover reads, \\"The Long Tail - Why the Future of Business Is Selling Less of More\\".  Call me simple-minded, but I did not truly comprehend the point he was trying to make in that statement alone.  However after reading the book, it all makes perfect sense!     In this book, Anderson explores the evolution of a new kind of economics, where people have unlimited choices as production and distribution costs have decreased drastically because of the internet and related technologies.  He discusses the historical models and practices, and then walks us through to the creation of new types of markets that we see today.  In the past, companies, marketers and advertisers were looking for the biggest \\"hits\\", the product or service that would appeal to the masses, and therefore, would great the most revenue and be the most profitable.  This model was based on the concept that there was limited \\"shelf\\" space and there were costs associated with carrying such items. In order to maximize profits, they sought to appeal to the masses.  This was exemplified in radio, tv, grocery stores, retailers, etc.  However, that principle focused only on the \\"head\\" of the curve (those that were perceived to be the biggest hits).  As you look further down the curve, the demand for more products tapers off, but in fact, never gets to zero! This is what is known as the Long Tail.  Essentially, there is some kind of demand for all products/services, no matter how niche and obscure they may seem.  The true shape is revealed when customers have infinite options.  As Anderson describes, the twentieth century was focused on the hits and in the twenty-first century, we are now focused on the niches.  He found that between a quarter and half of total revenues were from products that were not offered in the traditional brick-and-mortar store.  Information aggregators such as Google, Ebay and Amazon have played (and continue to play) a huge part in this revolution, and have made it possibly to find almost anything under the sun.     This has had major implications on our society, on the whole.  We now are accustomed to being able to find anything we want, whenever we want it.  We have hundreds of television stations, as compared to a few that were offered a few decades ago.  We have satellite radio. We have the internet, YouTube and millions of online sites that have \\"fragmented\\" our attention.  He also discusses that controversial idea of whether or not we, as consumers, do well with unlimited choices.  Anderson argues that consumers enjoy choices when they are able to seek out help about what to buy, look at recommendations, and have access to additional information.  In the online world, filters help us parse through the wanted and the unwanted, the relevant and the irrelevant, according to our individual wants and needs.     I thoroughly enjoyed this book and learning about his view of the Long Tail.  I like that his thoughts and theories were well supported and documented via years of research with executives and industry insiders.  It was great that he was given proprietary data from many of these cooperative companies to support this project. I would like to continue to read more from Anderson, and will seek out additional books and Wired articles he has authored.

And the building of community.     The notion of community -- and enchantment -- is the heart fire of Kawasaki's principles. Karloff's portrayal of the monster is an accomplished pantomime; a combination of pathos, innocence and rage that favourably compares to some of the greatest silent film performances from films like [[ASIN:B0000AINLS Sunrise]] and [[ASIN:0780022343 The Passion of Joan of Arc]]. Whichever version you choose is, therefore, entirely a matter of pricing, convenience and desired extras.<br /><br />Given its importance to the development of early horror cinema, any horror fan worth their salt should see Universal's \\"Frankenstein\\" at least once during their lifetime. The series is a great study of the evils and realities of war and rebellion and also the complexities of love.    Collins also has the rare ability to conjure a rich world with pleasingly stark prose; she doesn't spend much time on describing the world of Panem in insane detail, and yet she still manages to convey an incredibly clear vision of the dystopian future world. I also usually hate first-person perspective; I usually find it somewhat annoying to read, but Collins manages to use it to her advantage in all of the books.    The ending of Mockingjay is not fairy-tale-perfect by any means, but the subject matter with which it deals is not the sort of thing that lends itself to such endings. Not to be trite, but like many things in life, I think this series is a lot about the journey as opposed to the destination, and it very much succeeds on that front. Even though the ending isn't all wrapped up in a neat little package, taken as a whole it is immensely satisfying. I think if Collins had ended the series on a shiny-happy note that it would have downplayed the seriousness of the subject matter and the experience of the characters, and that the lessons that the books contain would be lessened as a result.    In any case, this is really one of the most enjoyable series of books I have ever read, and I will be thinking about them for a long time. Really great stuff.

0756405890|5.0|I'll just repost from my Goodreads review:    Reviewing this book has proven difficult, as I'm having trouble heaping upon it the praise it deserves without sounding ridiculously maudlin or fanboyish.     So, listen: this is a perfect example of skill and tone and form making ordinary ingredients into something extraordinary. I've read some tepid and negative reviews, and they all focus on either not liking the character or finding specific narrative elements trite or overdone (e.g., \\"The university setting was better in Harry Potter!\\" or \\"Oh, this is just another poor-boy-becomes-hero wish fulfillment fantasy\\").     To some extent, this is correct: it is about a poor boy that becomes a hero. But it's really about the sometimes-terrible price of notoriety; about the loneliness of the great; about the arrogance of the elite and the double-edged blessing of pride. It's about the curse of getting what you wish for and the ordeal of living in interesting times.     And it's written with such unrepentant enthusiasm and wit and self-awareness and *heart* that I just can't not like it. I suspect that when SkyNet takes over and we are hunted by the Machines, we will use dogs as the primary method of detecting Terminators and the suspect's opinion on \\"The Name of the Wind\\" as secondary verification of their humanity.     I try to remain mindful of the subjectivity of fiction and other people's tastes, but for this book, I cannot even feign evenhandedness or maturity: loving \\"The Name of the Wind\\" is the only Correct response.

B000I2JDEY|5.0|Sadly, there are very few films that are capable of eliciting a visceral reaction out of me. However, Mike Nichol's 1966 debut film, Who's Afraid of Virginia Woolf?, is such a powerful film on so many levels that I cannot imagine anybody watching it without being significantly moved. The film is based on Edward Albee's Tony Award winning play and covers the events of one very long and alcohol induced night. It follows the plight of an aging couple George (Richard Burton) and Martha (Elizabeth Taylor). When friends Nick (George Segal)  and Honey (Sandy Dennis) come over for a nightcap, they become caught in the middle of a series of games in which George and Martha take their pain and attempt to push each other to the breaking point. Albee's dialogue is explosive, both in terms of being emotionally charged and how it is projected, and is adeptly handled by the entire cast.. There is a brilliant mix of temperamental humor and anguish which includes one of my favorite lines, \\"There isn't an abomination award going that you haven't won.\\"   While Albee's superb script provides a well-built chassis for the film, it is Nichol's direction mixed with Haskell Wexler's brilliant cinematography that makes this film really take off. Often times the camera will capture the scene as if it were merely a fly on the wall. However, Nichols really knows the material and will zoom in to the point of intrusion when a character feels emotionally isolated or verbally backed into a corner. In one particularly distinguished scene that deviates from the play, Nichols captures George walking into the back of the house when the Martha and the guests are still conversing in the living room. Even though George is out of ear shot of the others, Nichols keeps the audio of their conversation going while he follows George. This illuminates the extent of Martha's deviance as George can still knows that Martha will be speaking ill of him even when he is not in her company. Also, in the same vein as a noir, Nichols uses mirrors to show how these characters never take a good hard look at themselves and what they have become. One is even shoved in the face of George at one point, yet he is too steeped in his own misery to even see the man on the other side.   The performances here are simply astounding by all players involved. Although Taylor would nab the Oscar for best actress, I really feel as though Burton was snubbed. While his character could have just been watered down to nothing more than a dispensary of caustic wit, Burton injects a sense of humanity into the character that is present in the smallest of gestures.    Historically, this film is bold as it was produced in some of the most impassioned years of the civil rights movement.  These years saw a mass movement to finally put an end to the anti-miscegenation laws that had previously prevented interracial marriages in America. This film shows that even white upper-middleclass marriages, which were seen as a bulwark against the decay of the human race, had their blemishes.   In the end, it is a beautiful film about a volatile relationship. It is about pain and the fiction that we manufacture in order to deal with it. The film isn't easy to watch, but if you do you will find that this is an absolutely astounding film.

B004M1MHMM|5.0|Because it's not very pretty and doesn't contain a lot of bells and whistles, I doubt that the Numark PT-01USB will score well with music purists. But if you're looking for an inexpensive way to get your record collection digitized, this is the USB turntable you're looking for.    It's not automatic, if that's important to you -- you have to do your own cueing and moving the arm after a side is played. The built-in speaker makes it easy to have it next to your computer and monitor the recording. If you're not in a position to listen, just plug in some headphones -- there are jacks for plugs both big and small.     The PT-01USB can handle all three record speeds, 33, 45 (look sharp, or you'll miss the adapter) and 78. While I was quite pleased with the overall sound quality, my first recording attempt was too low in volume. I found the solution in the user manual -- adjusting the turntable's gain control.    Though the PT-01USB works with both Mac and Windows, I suggest you ditch the software supplied in favor of a download of the free audio editing software Audacity -- the Mac version is outdated anyway. But that's the nastiest thing I can say about this product.    During its \\"down time,\\" the PT-01USB can be hooked up to \\"normal\\" speakers via its RCA plugs, and it's portable to boot! The dust cover doubles as a lid and there's a built-in handle -- add six D batteries, and you're mobile!    As an inexpensive replacement turntable that can digitize my vinyl, I say that the Numark PT-01USB fills the bill and already paid for itself.

1439107955|5.0|I cannot remember having read a book that has left me so emotionally exhausted as this.  It wasn't an easy read because the subject matter is not an easy one for a lay person -- though I suppose it isn't all that easy for a medical practitioner either.  An uneasy read it may have been but I found that I couldn't put it down.  And now that I have read it through to the end I am completely drained.  Some writing makes me laugh out loud; other pieces drive me to anger or frustration expressed audibly.  The Emperor of All Maladies is the only one I can remember in recent years that has brought me to tears aloud.    I can't pretend that I have understood or can remember all the science, all the treatments or the names of all the many people who appear in the book but the general message transmitted have been absorbed -- the flashes of brilliance among the scientists, the stubbornness of many of the medical practitioners, and so forth.  But it is Dr. Mukherjee's ability to remind his readers, sometimes at the most unexpected moments, that the story is a human story and that there are real people out there.  This is what makes this book so remarkable.  You are reading about scientists stuck in ruts and then someone has a spark of genius that ignites others or about surgeons and physicians locked into this procedure or that treatment regime -- and then suddenly a real human story is presented, usually no more than a page or two but enough to stop you dead in your tracks and realize that this isn't labs and hospitals but ordinary people.    This biography of an ever-changing disease is SO well written, SO readable, that in addition to its intrinsic interest it should stand an exemplar to other authors on how to convey ideas and knowledge on a difficult topic to a lay reradership.    This a highly engaging and extremely enlightening read. It is simply a wonderful book.

1401309666|4.0|The \\"Long Tail\\" by Chris Anderson began as an article in Wired magazine in 2004.  Anderson then expanded the ideas in his article into his book, which became a New York Times bestseller.          Anderson's theory of the long tail can be summarized as this:  Our current culture and economy are both increasingly shifting away from focusing on a relatively small number of hits (mainstream products, markets, songs, movies, etc) at the \\"head \\"of the demand curve.  Instead, we are moving toward a huge number of niches found in the tail of the curve.  Additionally, in this era without the constraints of physical shelf space and other bottlenecks of distribution, these \\"niche\\" type goods can be as economically attractive as the \\"hits\\" of mainstream fare.   However, simply offering as much choice as possible is not enough.  Anderson explains that in order for these \\"niche\\" items to be successful, and for the phenomenon of the \\"long tail\\" to work, there must be a significant demand for this new supply.  If not, the \\"long tail\\" will wither.  Anderson explains that this is due to the fact that the tail is not only measured by the \\"amount of variety available\\", but also the \\"number of people who gravitate towards these numerous choices\\".  As Anderson explains numerous times in this book, \\"the true shape of demand is revealed only when consumers are offered infinite choice.\\"   The section of the book that I particularly enjoyed was when Anderson explained the importance of \\"filters\\".  With so much choice now available over the web through services such as iTunes, Netflix, and Amazon, it can potentially be a challenge for a consumer something that satisfies their demand.  Anderson explains that the role of filters in today's long tail markets is shifting from \\"gatekeeper\\" to \\"advisor\\".  Instead of trying to predict tastes of the consumer, filters such as Google are measuring it.  Filters such as Netflix's customer recommendations help consumers reveal their likes and dislikes through their behavior.  Filters help create markets for things that are already available by stimulating demand for them.  While reading this section I couldn't help but think how much I rely on filters in today's world.  If there is a particular item that I want, I used massive filters such as Google to search for it.  Without filters, I do not feel that \\"long tail markets' could exist, because it would be too hard for the consumer to find the niche markets they are looking for.   Overall, I enjoyed the concept of this book.  I found it to be particularly relevant to today's marketplace and would recommend this to MBA students.  The only criticism I have of this book would be it could have been a little more concise.  I felt that Anderson repeated many of his points numerous times throughout the book.  However, I felt that many of these points were ahead of their time since they are still relevant 5 years after the book was originally published.  This impressed me since technology and the way we communicate has dramatically changed in that time period, which has further increased the \\"length of the tail\\".

She sings about how England has been haunted by the spectre of the bloody Gallipolli debacle. But the lyrics are none too subtle ('big guns shooting', 'orphaned children', 'limbs pointing upwards' ...). And they miss the biting irony that another war-obsessed brings to the theme. In Roger Waters' 'The Wall', 'The Final Cut' and 'Amused to Death', the pain and waste of war, and also its paradoxical, perverse beauty are brought into relief much more poignantly. PJ's musings on the whole leave me pretty cold. They are pedestrian, derivative and occasionally veer into unpalatable bombast (one might say that of Waters as well, of course). And yet she has shown in the past, with albums such as 'To Bring You My Love', 'Is This Desire' and 'White Chalk' that she is able to conjure visions of epic, biblical anger and mystery. My real frustration is with Katniss at the end of the novel. How could she do that? I thought that she had really grown to love him. Some of the things that she did and said only seemed to indicate how much she loved him. The fact that she said that if she was the one who lived she would never really leave the arena... the fact she risked her life to get the medicine at the feast... everything seemed to indicate that she loved him. And Gale, please, she had chance with him and she didn't take it. He has been in the friend zone too long. She shouldn't even be thinking about him in any way except \\"I can't wait to see my best friend again\\" way. I hope that she gets her act together (and quickly) and realizes that he is the one for her~<br /><br />Perhaps that thing I can identify with the most is the love Katniss has for her younger sister, Primrose. I am the oldest of four girls and I know that I would do anything for my sisters. I would die for them, if that is what was called for. I try to picture myself in Katniss' shoes, when she hears her sisters name be called, and all I can think is that I would have done the exact same thing. In my case, it would have meant certain death (I have no outdoor skills at all) but I would have rather died then spend the rest of my life knowing that I could have saved my sister and I didn't. The protective love that an older sibling feels for a younger one is expertly portrayed here.<br /><br />I can't imaginewhat it must have been like to be the parents of any of these characters. To be forced to watch on television while your child gets hunted down and killed. I can't imagine what it must be like to want your child to win, even then realizing that means that they must murder someone else's child. 0393078299|5.0|The practical success of quantum mechanics has enabled today's information-based economy in much the same way as the harnessing of steam did for the industrial revolution. The pioneers for both were few in number and whose long shadows were quickly covered by practitioners' incremental improvements accumulated to far reaching and long lasting impact on society's material culture. But quantum mechanics contains a paradox that is profoundly intellectual and with disturbing metaphysical aspects that are still not resolved to this day. This well written book details quantum mechanic's early history and background, from Max Plank's almost accidental discovery of an analytical method that explained what were then considered minor details in physics, including the birth of theoretical physics that coincided with the collapse of classical physic's explanation of the material universe as then understood, and, not least, the clash of two giants, Einstein and Bohr and their differing worldviews, as the paradox appeared and deepened when evolving analytical and manipulative skills became more powerful. Fast paced and much like a mystery with several levels of narrative. One of the most notable is the fact that the cast of characters and their work was extremely small, unknown and unheralded until Einstein became an international celebrity. Another is the power of imagination, long before science was spelled with a capital S, through the famous thought experiments made famous by Einstein, that brought understanding based on mathematical reasoning and without needing experimental proof. The players' egos, foibles, follies and insightful flashes also are part of the story, and how they sorted themselves into two camps reflecting the two opposing world views that provide the crux of the paradox. Einstein profoundly and deeply believed in the independent existence of the material universe, while Bohr held just as profoundly and deeply that there is no absolute reality beyond what can be conjured by observation. This highly readable book covers not just some physicists' quibbles, but the vista to our understanding of the universe as we comprehend it, from the unimaginably physically small realm of quantum mechanics to the seemingly limitless vastness of the cosmos.n be conjured by observation. Lets look at J.K. Rowlings. After the first several books came out, she became well known. Hence she came out with book number five, which was one of the most painful books I have ever read in my life. It took me two - three months to get through it. Luckily I am such a faithful follower that I returned, and book six redeemed it. But even book seven, you were left wondering how much better it would have been if there hadn't been pressure from publishers and expectations from adoring fans. Another example of this can be seen in Stephanie Meyer. The first book of twilight was amazing... the second two were okay, but the forth book, when she became a megastar, was horrible. It took twists that made me vomit in my mouth. I stopped reading it at page 400 because, even considering it was about vampires and warewolves, it had crossed a line so unbelievable for the world she had created, that I couldn't accept it anymore. So Dan Brown is in good company... lets just hope that he his next novel is better.<br /><br />Now, all of that said and done, I still give the book a 3.5/5 stars. Everything could have been explained to Langdon without going there. And upon finding out what the great secret word was, I was left wondering why was it so secret? What was so important about that secret that it was worth risking everything to protect. It was a huge let down. And I was also left wondering how Peter Solomon seemed to know everything about it, when no one had decoded the pyramid before.<br /><br />Overall, this novel was a huge let down that I am still trying to recover from. But I guess that with all the hype surrounding it, I shouldn't have expected as much as I did... It wasn't really fair to Dan Brown. So the question is, if Dan Brown puts out another novel, will I rush to the bookstore and pick it up and read it??? Answer: In a heartbeat!

Unfortunately this particular area of disaster movies has been seldom explored ever since the boom in the VFX industry. Battle : LA promises to bring realism to the alien world invasion backdrop and it succeeds to a certain point. Except, mashing up of two different genres--war movie and alien disaster movie--renders it a rather confused pile of yesterday's pasta.<br /><br />Taking obvious cues from much superior movies like Black Hawk Down and Saving Private Ryan, Jonathan Liebesman tried to do what directors seldom reflect on in disaster movies--the psychology of the combatants and the relationship dynamics within the squad. B003UESJH4|5.0|I saw this film, which I have pre-ordered on Amazon, three times already; and I daresay I shall watch it many more times. \\"The King's Speech\\" is adult entertainment in the most noble sense of the expression. The script is outstanding in its intelligence, and if there were an award for the best use of profanity in context without giving offence, I would definitely give it to \\"The King's Speech.\\" The film is simply splendid! Without benefit of car chases, explosions, or overly-spectacular CGI, the audience is riveted--indeed, held spellbound--for 119 minutes by a simple scenario: a wife seeks professional help for her husband's speech impediment; he undergoes therapy and with persistent practice, he overcomes--if not conquers--his problem despite the low expectations of the public and, most of all, of himself. That the wife is the Duchess of York and the husband is the future King of England is irrelevant and at the same time empowering: the story contains a universal truth; the future king, like the kings of Greek tragedy, represents every man as he learns through suffering.<br /><br />Since the actors have been deservedly praised to the skies, I shall comment only upon a few points that impressed me. Colin Firth's portrayal of Bertie is so believable, because he conveys the unimaginable physical tension of a man whose thoughts are entrapped within his body; and paradoxically, Firth does so with ease; there is nothing forced about his portrayal. Geoffrey Rush, who plays his therapist Lionel Logue with sensitivity and humour, is certainly Firth's equal. It is a shame that a double Oscar could not have been awarded. Both Firth and Rush provide an equilibrium that would certainly be upset if either one were taken away (or if one were one replaced by another actor). I was especially struck by Rush's ability to portray a not-very-good amateur, when he tries out for the part of Richard III; he suggests an ever-so slight hamminess without actually hamming it up. A difficult challenge for any actor, much less one of his stature! I also thought that Helena Bonham Carter, who exhibits a combination of privilege and propriety tempered by kindness, was a perfect foil for Jennifer Ehle (Rush's wife), who exhibits similar characteristics and also understands the boundaries of decorum: a wonderful moment comes when a surprised Ehle politely asks the royal couple if they will be staying for dinner, and the Duchess graciously thanks her and cites a previous engagement as if it were a necessary annoyance.<br /><br />One of the things that British filmmakers do so well is to cast supporting roles with top-drawer actors, disguising them thoroughly. I found myself reading the credits the first time around and exclaiming to my friend: \\"Good Heavens! Anthony Andrews was playing Stanley Baldwin. I certainly didn't recognise him!\\" Claire Bloom also inhabited the persona of Queen Mary so thoroughly that I didn't know it was she until the end credits. It took a few minutes for me to recognise Derek Jacobi as the Archbishop, and I recognised Michael Gambon as George V only by his voice, and a certain look in his eyes. Each submerges his own personality into his respective role. Unfortunately, the same cannot be said for Timothy Spall, who provides what I consider the only false note, giving me the impression that he is no other than Timothy Spall, pouting, and acting Churchillian. I found it most expedient to avert my eyes whenever he came on the screen and focus on all the other things to love about this movie.<br /><br />This film combines pathos with humour. My friend and I were blubbering (as silently as possible) and laughing (out loud) all the way through it. And because of the skills of Colin Firth and Jeffrey Rush in conveying the feelings of Bertie and Lionel, our emotional responses were spontaneous and not prised out of us, as happens too often in films nowadays. I was especially moved as the film reached its climax, knowing the tragedy that was about to strike Europe, the people of England, and indeed the world. 0446691437|5.0|Rarely will a nonfiction book get me so engrossed that time stands still as I read it.  But The War of Art was that rare book.  Only 192 pages, I read it on a Sunday morning over a cup of coffee (or two).  Pressfield wrote this book for me, the procrastinator. And he spelled out in plain language what makes me procrastinate:  Resistance.    Resistance is that thing that keeps us from doing anything we want to do, pursue any creative project (including writing), diet or exercise, making a commitment to overcome a bad habit or addiction.  Any pursuit, really, that doesn't give immediate gratification. Leave it to Resistance to make sure there are plenty of other things we find to do instead.    My favorite quote from the book is this:  \\"Procrastination is the most common manifestation of Resistance because it's the easiest to rationalize. We don't tell ourselves, `I'm never going to write my symphony.'  Instead we say, `I am going to write my symphony; I'm just going to start tomorrow.'\\"  Wow!  Me in a nutshell.    Part practicality and part spirituality, The War of Art spells out what Resistance is and how it lures us into battle and kicks our butts if we let it.  Part Two delves into how to battle Resistance by turning from amateur to Pro (which is all about attitude, not cash flow).    The third part of the book wasn't as interesting to me, as it was where the \\"spiritual\\" came in, but it gives a peek into Pressfield's early writing career through his first big success, the writing of the book, The Legend of Bagger Vance, which later became a movie starring Will Smith, which is quite inspirational.  Some readers might be interested in his talk of Muses and angels, Self and Ego, but I started to drift about this time.  A few sections of Part Three held my attention, especially the part about fear.    However, the first two thirds of the book were well worth the money.  I would recommend this to any writer (any creative person, really), who wants to know what causes the procrastination that keeps them from doing what they really long to do.  I'll be rereading  The War of Art more than once, I know.
