B0034G4OUO|4.0|Ten years behind bars, and he's so antsy. You see the Rock's character - anonymously labeled \\"Driver\\" - as he restlessly paces in his prison cell, waiting out his last morning in stir, and you know something festers on his mind. Any musings as to whether he's truly been rehabilitated is put to rest when he erupts out the penitentiary and straight away visits an office and guns down a telemarketer. That's cold-blooded. That's one name off his list (yes, there is a list).<br /><br />It's good to see the Rock straying from those sappy family friendly Disney flicks. Good to see him return to his roots and bolstering his action hero street creds. FASTER works off the conventions of a B-movie revenge thriller, and good performances from Dwayne Johnson and Billy Bob Thornton elevate this thing. There are moments of pure cool cinema, like that point of view perspective from the other side of the car's speedometer which allows us a peek out at the seething face of the Rock. I don't think I've seen a revenge thriller this lean and raw and entertaining since Mel Gibson's PAYBACK more than a decade ago.<br /><br />FASTER  is told mainly from three perspectives: the Driver, the Killer, and the drug junkie Cop, with the latter two trying to catch up to the Driver as he continues to eliminate those what done him wrong, those what murdered his brother. Just about every character here is one-dimensional, but Johnson manages to lend nuance to his laconic role. Billy Bob Thornton, as he usually does, adds a little somethin' somethin' to his scruffy, 10-days-to-retirement police detective.<br /><br />One peculiar choice is the film's decision to devote so much camera time and back story to Oliver Jackson-Cohen's elegant contract hit man. He sees in Driver that one challenge he's been looking for, the one guy who may be faster than he is and that gets his competitive juices flowing. But, frankly, ultimately, I didn't think this hit man was all that interesting. He's what passes for shallow glitter. I did get a laugh though that Jackson-Cohen's biggest peeve there at the end seems to be that he's just not getting the professional courtesy he feels he deserves from the Rock. Jackson-Cohen certainly adds an element of quirk, and it doesn't hurt that his personal arc presents the stunning Maggie Grace as eye candy. Grace, Carla Gugino, and Moon Bloodgood somewhat take the edge off the testosterone level, never mind that Gugino herself plays a tough cop detective.<br /><br />Dwayne Johnson strides across the screen as a simmering, single-minded force of nature. He relentlessly drives the film, ratcheting up the pace of the story, and maybe it's a good thing after all that Jackson-Cohen's posh assassin gets his moments because this allows the viewing audience to breathe a bit. I did feel that the film backed away a bit from completely portraying Driver as the hard-bitten ex-convict out for vengeance come hell or high water. That one drawn out sequence with Driver and the pastor sort of killed the buzz. Dang that Bible-thumping. Still, FASTER satisfies your jonesing for taut, hard-core anti-hero revenge flicks. B003Y5H55Q|4.0|I was a bit hesitant about seeing Paul from the critical reviews and from the trailer, but found myself enjoying it quite a bit when I took a chance and saw it. There's a lot to see in Paul, and much like Galaxy Quest (1999), Paul both mocks its subject matter while at the same time treating it with both understanding and great affection.  A lot of talent went into Paul and it shows.  Directed by Greg Mottola (Superbad, Adventureland, TV's Arrested Development) and written by Simon Pegg (Hot Fuzz, Shaun of the Dead, TV's Spaced) and Nick Frost, Paul hits all the right comic notes, skewering its target audience knowingly and lovingly.<br /><br />What makes Paul work is that, at its heart, it's the ultimate scifi road trip. Graeme (Simon Pegg) and Clive (Nick Frost), two major scifi/comics/UFOs fanboys from the UK, are visiting the US on a pilgrimage of sorts, hitting the biggest scifi/comics/gaming convention on the West Coast and making the grand tour of all the famous UFO-lore sites (Area 51, Roswell, 'The Black Mailbox', etc) in a huge honkin' RV.  While on the road out in the desert, they witness a car spin out of control and crash. When they hurry to see if they can help, they come face to face with the classic little green alien with huge head and enormous oval eyes. Except that he's smoking a cigarette, introduces himself as 'Paul', and talks like Seth Rogen (mainly because he's voiced by Seth Rogen, absolutely the perfect choice for this role). Paul tells Graeme (Clive's fainted) of his need to escape the government people coming after him and the road trip is on.<br /><br />The cast is great.  Pegg and Frost are perfect in their roles as fanboys who've been buddies since childhood and have never really grown up, Frost's Clive hoping to finish his breakthrough novel and Pegg's Graeme doing the illustrations for it (the triple-breasted alien woman on the cover is a running gag that keeps getting funnier). Rogen gives Paul his personality, quite literally, making him a fairly unique creation: the stoner/slacker alien who's gone native in a lot of ways but has decided it's time to phone home (largely because the powers that be have apparently decided to quit merely picking his brain and remove it instead). Jason Bateman is great as the deadpan Agent Zoil, the 'man in black' hot on Paul's trail.  Bill Hader and Joe LoTruglio are excellent comic foils as his low-grade backups, Agents Haggard and O'Reilly. Jeffrey Tambor is a scene-stealer in his role as the arrogant fanboys' icon, Adam Shadowchild. Kristen Wiig adds a lot to the comic mix as Ruth Buggs, a repressed Christian fundamentalist (she wears a t-shirt with Jesus shooting Darwin through the head) whose close-encounter with Paul ends up liberating her in a completely over-the-top way (think Jessica from True Blood, just with aliens instead of vampires). And there's a major cameo that's held in reserve until the climactic scene, but once it's revealed, your reaction will be \\"Of course! Who else?\\"<br /><br />Another fun part of Paul is picking up all of the movie references, and Paul contains a _lot_ of them, sometimes in lines that are either quotes or parodies of quotes, sometimes in scenes or visuals, from just about every major scifi movie imagineable.  Star Wars, Star Trek, Close Encounters of the Third Kind, Raiders of the Lost Ark, Aliens, Back To The Future, E.T., Predator,  Starman.  You name it, it's probably in there somewhere.  My personal favorite is a scene with a country & western band in a biker bar, playing their version of about the last thing you'd ever expect to hear in that context. I really want to get the DVD when it comes out so I can go back and catch all the ones I missed the first time.<br /><br />There are a few places where things do drag a bit, usually where the toilet/grossout humor (it's Seth Rogen after all) goes on a little too long. But for the most part Paul keeps things going fast and furious, and the cast are so into their roles that even when you can sense the joke coming, it's still funny when it hits you.<br /><br />Highly recommended for anyone who loves sci-fi/alien films and for anyone who's ever been a fanboy of anything.ke coming, it's still funny when it hits you. 1401302378|3.0|In his book \\"The Long Tail,\\" Chris Anderson illustrates that the future of business will rely on selling less of more.  By utilizing the statistical concept of Pareto Tails, Anderson argues that companies should focus on selling niche products rather than the traditional business approach of primarily selling popular products deemed as \\"hits.\\"  The author depicts \\"hit\\" products as the area under the head of a statistical curve with the highest amount of concentration points and niche products as the \\"long tail\\" of low frequency events that never seems to end. Anderson's main concept relies on the theory that the aggregate total of the low frequency events (niche products) will be greater than the densely populated head of the graph (hit products).  However, this concept is only possible given today's digital business structure.  Traditional brick and mortar retail stores rely heavily on the 80/20 business rule that states that 80% of revenue will come from 20% of products.  Anderson argues that due to democratization of production tools, minimization of distribution costs, and communication between online consumers, retailers should focus on the 98% Rule.  The 98% Rule states that 98% of all possible choices are chosen at least once.  While traditional retailers are limited by space and resources to carry only the most popular products, digital retailers are able to focus on selling a variety of niche products.  In the book, Anderson uses a variety of companies such as Amazon, Rhapsody, and NetFlix to exemplify his thesis.  He emphasizes the importance of filters to help direct consumers towards appropriate products, and claims that recommendations from other consumers are a vital component to the future of business.<br />Overall, I thought that \\"The Long Tail\\" was a good read for any business student or professional.  The book become relatively redundant once I understood his main objective, but his culturally relevant examples helped bring his points to life.  Anderson makes many interesting points regarding both the future of business and the general trends of society as a whole.  However, I do think he undervalues the importance of \\"hit\\" products.  While I understand the significance of catering towards niche markets, \\"hit\\" products will always be wildly successful.  Must-have products establish a commonality amongst groups of and are typically the reason consumers are searching sites such as Amazon in the first place.  Only once a consumer is drawn to the internet for a product, can filters and consumer noise along the long tail be effective.  Catering towards numerous niche markets may enable companies to be timeless and never die with a particular fad, but I do believe there is still plenty of merit behind producing a \\"hit.\\"son makes many interesting points regarding both the future of business and the general trends of society as a whole.  However, I do think he undervalues the importance of \\"hit\\" products.  While I understand the significance of catering towards niche markets, \\"hit\\" products will always be wildly successful.  Must-have products establish a commonality amongst groups of and are typically the reason consumers are searching sites such as Amazon in the first place.  Only once a consumer is drawn to the internet for a product, can filters and consumer noise along the long tail be effective.  Catering towards numerous niche markets may enable companies to be timeless and never die with a particular fad, but I do believe there is still plenty of merit behind producing a \\"hit.\\"

030746363X|2.0|If you are a young, hip tech oriented person the odds are good that you've heard a lot about this book and it's author. Say what you want about him, but he's a master of marketing and self promotion and did a fantastic job of getting the word out. I think, for that reason, this book will appeal to a lot of folks in the tech circles, as they look up to one of their own who has made it big. But can it justify the hype? I don't think so. There's nothing really wrong with this book. If you follow the diet advice you will lose weight. How? Through calorie restriction, just like every other diet. Yes yes, the type of food you eat makes a difference, but that doesn't change the fact that you are still restricting calories.    I'm going to mostly focus on the diet here, because I think it's what most people will buy the book for, and I think a lot of the rest is mostly fluff (I had friends doing the uberman sleep schedule in the late 90's). Here's the thing, his diet plan is just a low carb diet. There is quite a bit of research out there that supports the idea that low carb is the best way to lose fat, that insulin response is important, etc. I tend to agree with these findings. However, it's not the closed case that low carb promoters will have you believe. The jury is still out, and at the end of the day any calorie restricted diet is likely to be effective (though potentially some more than others).    There is one big problem with a low carb diet however, and that is, it's difficult to eat enough. If you are shooting for say 1,800 calories a day that amounts to 10 cups of broccoli and 5 chicken breasts. Now can you imagine eating that in one day? Heck no! When you limit the carbs and stick to clean, lean protein and whole veggies, it's *really* hard to consume enough calories. For that reason, the low carbers all recognize you've got to do something to make up for the lack of carbs and add calories back in (as consuming too few calories can be detrimental to weight loss). The Atkins camp does this by encouraging you to eat lots of fat, either fatty meats or dairy products. Smother that chicken in cheese, have some bacon, etc. The Paleo camp has you do it by adding in nuts and small amounts of fruits. Tim has you do it by adding in beans. Those are your 3 low carb camps today and out of all of them I tend to side with Atkins as 1) it's the tastiest option and 2) it's the only one that doesn't add significant carbs to the diet.    This is where Tim loses me a bit. Beans are a carb. Sure they have protein and fiber, but they're still a carb and have a glycemic index similar to rice. Now, I don't tend to think that cutting out all carbs is wise, so I'm just fine with this. But I think it's important to note that this is not a revolutionary diet, it's just a theme on something that has been popular for a while. If you like beans, and believe that consuming fat is bad for you (I don't), then this is probably the diet for you.    That said, after weighing it for a few days, I went from an initial excitement to try it, to deciding this just isn't for me. There are two big reasons for this... First, I hate beans. I could probably come up with some recipes for beans that I could eat a few times a week and not suffer too much, but the thought of eating beans 3 times a day is just too much for me. I would have serious compliance issues and I'm pretty sure it would fail for me. After all, if a diet requires you to eat something you don't enjoy eating, you won't stick to it. And with his diet plan, if you don't eat the beans, you won't be consuming enough calories, plain and simple. If, however, you're the type that salivates at the thought of a 3 bean salad, and could eat black beans plain out of the can, then you'll be quite pleased with this plan. If you love to whip up recipes to make beans delicious, you're right at home as well. For me, I need things quick and simple, and there's just nothing I can do to beans that is quick and simple which I will want to eat...    The second problem is that this diet is still a diet. For most people, in my opinion, it is not maintainable in the long term. Were I to force myself to go on this diet until I reach my weight loss goals, I would give it up the instant I hit my weight goal, because of that whole bean hatred thing. At that point I'd likely go back to normal eating habits and gain the weight back. I am a firm believer that rather than going on a specific diet to achieve a short term weight loss goal, you should instead build a healthy diet you can stick with indefinitely and simply work on portion control to lose weight. For most of us these changes are quite simple and involve giving up high calorie beverages, greatly reducing simple carbs and sticking to complex carbs (true whole grain bread, brown rice, oatmeal, etc), and getting plenty of lean protein and veggies. If your diet is realistic, and something that you can follow for the rest of your life, I believe it will be far more successful. Sure, it might not get you the quickest results, but it will get you the most long term results.     I do enjoy his advice about having a binge day, however again, that's not exactly a new idea. From personal experience I've found that while having a binge day does lead to an instant weight gain (2-3 lbs for me), over the long term I lose weight faster. That instant weight gain is mostly glycogen being stored up by your body (commonly known as water weight), not fat, and will quickly be depleted to be replaced by further weight loss through the next week. While tracking my weight I've found that I lose weight much faster with a binge day, and if I haven't had one but my weight loss has stalled, having a free day kicks it back in gear. Of course we are all different, but it's something I highly recommend trying to see how your body reacts.    Overall, it's a fine book. The advice is solid, and if the diet plan fits with your tastes you will have good results. But please, keep in mind that it's nothing revolutionary and you need to honestly assess whether a diet of lean meat, veggies and lots of beans is something you'll realistically be able to stick to. If not, there is no point in doing it. If it's not a diet you are likely to stick to, it's not going to work, no matter what kind of amazing results the author may present.

0307408833|4.0|5/6 of my family is vegan, and 1/2 of us have a wheat intolerance.  I got this book just in time for my oldest's 17th birthday, and I was really excited to make the Paradise Cake.  And... it was okay, bordering on kind of dry.  We put it away, then discovered the next morning that it had improved with refrigeration- both softer and sweeter.  Go figure.  That motivated me to give some of the other recipes a try.<br /><br />My daughter tried the Vanilla Cupcakes as they were written up in the book.  They were not that great.  But I raised my eyebrow a bit when I saw how many recipes used applesauce.  Years ago, I learned in an issue of Cooking Light that if you're going to use applesauce the best way to do it is to drain it, usually by spreading it out on paper towels (I use old paper bags).  The amount of moisture that comes out is impressive, and as most whole-grain bakers know, moisture paradoxically makes baked goods denser.  I tried both the Chocolate and Vanilla Cupcakes using my technique, and the results were fabulous.  (So much so that I couldn't save enough cupcakes to make crumbs that time around.)  Why McKenna doesn't mention this, I don't know.<br /><br />The frosting recipes are good- easily the best I've seen without confectioners sugar- but some people are not going to love the taste of the soymilk powder.  I've cut back with good results.  Experienced cooks are going to recommend the technique as the same one used to make mayonnaise.  I will note that even after chilling the frosting is still more of a thick sauce- until you stir just a touch more liquid into it.  For vanilla, it was food coloring, for chocolate just a touch of coffee did the trick.  If you want pure vanilla frosting, maybe some extra vanilla will do the trick.<br /><br />I have nothing but good things to say about the biscuits, johnny cakes (a new family favorite), lemon poppy cake, corn bread and banana bread.  So nice to have baked goods like this for breakfast and snacks.  The brownies are also delicious.  Don't be freaked out by the specific instructions about what size baking container to use.  The brownies can be baked in a brownie pan, not just muffin tins, and the loaf cakes can be baked... in a traditional loaf pan, not just the oddly-sized, small ones she specifies.  (And of course they also work very nicely as muffins too.)<br /><br />The ingredients she calls for are expensive (welcome to wheat-free baking), so most people are going to be loath to experiment.  As such, it would have been nice if McKenna had explained some of her recipe choices, particularly the need for hot water.  Does hot water activate the baking powder or soda more quickly?  Is it safer to use because you're not working with as much gluten?  I don't know, and an explanation would have been appreciated.<br /><br />Still, with some tweaks, this is a really great cookbook for those who can't eat wheat and don't want to use sugar. 0756404738|4.0|Characters    This book continues the story as Kvothe records his life story while talking to his apprentice Bast and Chronicler.  Kvothe doesn't really undergo much of a growth arc throughout this book, but this is understandable when given some thought.  Even though we do change as we grow older, looking back on our life, as Kvothe is doing, it is very difficult to see this change over time in ourselves.  The story also spends a fair amount of time dealing with the relationship between Kvothe and Denna as well as the different people Kvothe interacts with at the University and abroad, some of the scenes with Elodin lead to very interesting exchanges as well.    Setting    Kvothe spends the first third of the book recounting more of his time at the University, which makes sense as it was the major part of his life at the time he is recounting, but it was irritating to me at times.  From the very beginning of the first book we are told of some of his deeds throughout the world, and unfortunately most of these actions don't take place at the University, so we need to get him away from the University before we can get to hear about all of those adventures.  Eventually we get to several other large towns and eventually to lands well away from the university and even into other realms.    Plot    Much like the first book, there are two different plots going on at once, there is the frame story where Kvothe is reciting his story to Chronicler and Bast as well as the story he is telling them.  The flashback story is entertaining especially towards the later parts of the book where we get to see how Kvothe helps to shape his identity by nudging exaggerations of some of stories as well as just through his actions.  Rothfuss does an excellent job of showing how stories get blown out of proportion to the extent that they do.  While Kvothe is a very smart character who has a lot of knowledge about the workings of the world, he is nowhere near as powerful as many of the storiestold about him make him out to be.    At the end of The Name of the Wind and at times in The Wise Man's Fear, we are brought back to present day and shown some of the problems Kvothe is facing.  As I finished NotW and several times in this story, I was more interested in the frame story than the story that Kvothe is telling about his past.  We are shown all of these fantastic things that Kvothe has done through his life, all that he has learned and studied, and he is shown to be somewhat depressed and reluctant to deal with life's problems.  What happened to Kvothe to cause him to get this way?  Where is Denna?  As interesting as Kvothe's past is, I want to know more about what is going on now.  I understand that we're getting there in the story, but I suppose I'm impatient.    Enjoyment    Although I have my complaints about the story that I said above, I still liked the book.  While I didn't tear through it as fast as I have some other books, I did really enjoy the story as well as reading about Kvothe's cleverness in dealing with his problems.  There were times when it slowed down a bit, however, those were mostly in the beginning when he was still at the University.  The frame story left me wanting to read more about Kvothe because I want to know what happened to the cocky kid who wanted all the knowledge in the world and how he came to settle as an innkeeper.  I will be eagerly awaiting the next book and highly suggest the series to anyone who enjoys fantasy books.    Overall Grade    8/10
